<div id="review_2408832655" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2408832655" />
    <a title="Tbfrank" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/901924-tbfrank"><img alt="Tbfrank" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2408832655?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tbfrank" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tbfrank" href="/user/show/901924-tbfrank">Tbfrank</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062694591" href="/book/show/38633526-vita-nostra">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2408832655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1036763596563271268">The "world" as hypertext...a product of human language<br/><br/>Creation myths take various forms, one of which states the world does not exist until the the objects within it are given names. A corollary states that knowing and uttering a True name conveys control over that object or being. Persons capable of wielding this power have been called shamans, magicians, gods, and writers. This particular theme makes a frequent appearance in fantastic literature from Le Guin's The Word for World is Forest to R</span>
  <span id="freeText1036763596563271268" style="display:none">The "world" as hypertext...a product of human language<br /><br />Creation myths take various forms, one of which states the world does not exist until the the objects within it are given names. A corollary states that knowing and uttering a True name conveys control over that object or being. Persons capable of wielding this power have been called shamans, magicians, gods, and writers. This particular theme makes a frequent appearance in fantastic literature from Le Guin's The Word for World is Forest to Rothfuss's The Name of the Wind, and Graedon's The Word Exchange, or Fforde's The Eyre Affair, to name only a few.<br /><br />In Vita Nostra, the idea is taken further. The world and its contents, reality as we experience it, is considered as information. To understand and master the ability to manipulate these informational objects, each person must be deconstructed and reconstructed through a series of mental and emotional gymnastics. Naturally, this requires education and training in a place of higher learning, The Institute of Special Technologies - another common theme. As easy as it is to make the comparison to Hogwarts, living in the dormitories was closer to the cells in the gulag in terms of comfort if not the number of occupants.<br /><br />Seventeen-year-old Sasha is selected by a "mysterious stranger" as a prospective adept and given a series of unpleasant tasks. Manipulated by an irrational fear of the consequence of non-compliance, she falls under the control of a being Shasa is certain is not human, possibly a demon or an alien. She believes that Farit, who becomes her adviser at the Institute, can affect the lives of her loved ones, first her mother, then her mother's new husband, and her baby brother. As she progresses, Sasha learns a person is not a wholly self-contained entity - they are reflected in those persons and objects around them in various degrees - and that to love is to be vulnerable, hence fearful. Farit admits he motivated Sasha by focusing her fears.<br /><br />The idea of objects manipulated through human action at a distance, sometimes called magic, sometimes psychic abilities, is another common theme reflecting the very human desire (and belief) to affect objects and events remotely. This can produce positive or negative results. Saying, doing, thinking, believing, or praying the right or approved or acceptable things ought to prevent the worst from happening. Conversely...<br /><br />As Sasha progresses, she begins to appreciate the differences in the mundane nature of human conversation; how so much is essentially filler, void of informational content yet uttering the right or the wrong words can produce a profound effect. <br /><br />[Doctor Who comes to mind as a fictional example - the 2012 Christmas episode where Clara Oswald, needing the Doctor's help, is told to select a single word that will interest him. She chooses "Pond," the source of her concern and incidentally the surname of The Doctor' previous companion.]<br /><br />Sasha also begins to physically change, to undergo metamorphosis, raising another theme, what it means to be human. She senses that some of the professors were once human while others are not and seem to have little compassion for their charges if any feelings at all. She fears becoming less human or non-human, unwilling to relinquish a human-centric view of reality.<br /><br />The novel is broadly based in magic realism reminiscent of Gabriel Garcia Marques and Ray Bradbury. The multiple elements and layers hid the authors' goal very well, making the reader uncertain if the students were being trained to fight an alien war or being prepared for translation to a computerized form. Set in a remote village named Torpa, the name is found as a district in Sweden and another in India. Why the main street was named Sacco and Vanzetti, two Italian anarchists whose execution in 1927 produced world-wide protests, is not revealed.<br /><br />Sasha, thought to be an imperative verb - a command - is discovered to be a password during her final exam. She appears to be confronted by the creator, perhaps another being similar to herself or God, and becomes afraid. As a password, she has the power to unlock a new information matrix, to rebuild the existing or a new reality based on her first words. She hesitates...and having read the ending several times, what she decided to do, whether she passed or failed, is murky. I like to think she "chose wisely."<br /><br />Post Script: China Mieville's The Last Days of New Paris, this line jumped out:<br />"[I]n the right context you can make words do all kinds of things."</span>
  <a data-text-id="1036763596563271268" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100850983-kat-nova?shelf=standalone">standalone</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3309926256" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4803011632442290455"> <i> “To live is to be vulnerable”</i><br/><br/>I'm so incredibly mind fucked right now I don’t even know where to start…. This review is probably going to be a bit messy. Like my mind.<br/><br/>This was probably the weirdest book I’ve ever read. Let’s start there. The way “magic” works here is so unique, it’s not even magic, it’s kind of a science of life? I still haven’t quite figured it out, I’m confused but in a very good way. Our protagonist, Sasha, had to work hard for her powers and I loved watching her do it. It d</span>
  <span id="freeText4803011632442290455" style="display:none"> <i> “To live is to be vulnerable”</i><br /><br />I'm so incredibly mind fucked right now I don’t even know where to start…. This review is probably going to be a bit messy. Like my mind.<br /><br />This was probably the weirdest book I’ve ever read. Let’s start there. The way “magic” works here is so unique, it’s not even magic, it’s kind of a science of life? I still haven’t quite figured it out, I’m confused but in a very good way. Our protagonist, Sasha, had to work hard for her powers and I loved watching her do it. It did not come easy to her, she cried and she went through all sorts of emotions to become what she needed to be. The Sasha at the beginning of the book is a completely different person compared to the Sasha at the end of it. Obviously, I mean three years pass throughout this. You get to watch this confused girl grow into a flawed but bright young woman.<br /><br />This freaking school was brutal. They forced these kids to come and study a bunch of weird crap and if they didn’t then bad stuff happened. My first impression, naturally, was that this was a cult of sorts. That these kids were getting brainwashed. I was creeped but fascinated. <br /><br />It was quite refreshing to not have romance be the focus of the book. Vita Nostra wasn’t really character driven but it also wasn’t plot driven, it was somewhere in between… Concept driven? Is that a thing?<br /><br />The writing built these fascinating images in my head and it slightly reminded me of the way Laini Taylor’s works made me feel. I’m not saying you’ll like this if you like her stuff but try it out if you do.<br /><br />As for the audiobook, which I listened to as I read, it was quite lovely. I would definitely recommend it. Without it, I would probably be a lot more confused. The narrators' voice was relaxing. She did, however, mess up most of the Russian names but it’s fine.,. I’m used to it lol</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2630459816" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2630459816" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57090347-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1474188682p2/57090347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2630459816?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/57090347-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062694591" href="/book/show/38633526-vita-nostra">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57090347-maria?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2630459816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11547650441825713420">This book is slow paced, philosophical, existential and it has a sort of dreary desperation throughout. But it's also a mysterious YA fantasy book set in Russia. It's dark and bleak, and can probably be described as a more mature wizarding school book.<br/><br/>It's definitely not a light read, although the language is simple and easy enough. For me, this has been one of those immersive, engrossing reads that wraps me up in it's specific, unique atmosphere and won't let go until I've read the last word - </span>
  <span id="freeText11547650441825713420" style="display:none">This book is slow paced, philosophical, existential and it has a sort of dreary desperation throughout. But it's also a mysterious YA fantasy book set in Russia. It's dark and bleak, and can probably be described as a more mature wizarding school book.<br /><br />It's definitely not a light read, although the language is simple and easy enough. For me, this has been one of those immersive, engrossing reads that wraps me up in it's specific, unique atmosphere and won't let go until I've read the last word - and maybe not even then. <br /><br />The main character, Sasha, is a straight A student, just about to finish high school. While she is on holiday with her mother, she is discovered by Farit Kozhennikov, a man with scary, supernatural abilities. Through rigorous testing she is chosen to study at an isolated, far away school. She has no say in the matter. The book is very mysterious. Both Sasha and the reader are mostly left in the dark, until we're close to the end. <br /><br />The book is a bit Kafkaesque in that Sasha and the other students are being given incomprehensible tasks by scary, unapproachable authority figures. Magic, or supernatural abilities, are something far darker and more grown up than in <i>Harry Potter</i>. It moves more towards metaphysics. It's about how you view reality, and about molding your brain and consciousness into seeing the world differently. No wands, spells or potions. It's all about your own mind. <br /><br />In the middle of a lot of mind bending, metaphysical ideas and exercises, are real teenagers, their free will pretty much taken away, trying to make it through this very demanding education, not even knowing if they will survive or what they will become at the end of it. The book has some clear YA qualities, and the teenagers make teenage mistakes. This added depth to the book, in my view. It makes the scary school Sasha goes to seem more real, when the people in it are ordinary, struggling teenagers. <br /><br />I'm uncertain whether this is a good or bad translation, but I recognize the type of writing a little bit from other translated Russian books. The language doesn't flow as well as a book originally written in English, it might sometimes feel like it has immature or flat language, but at the same time, you can see that there's plenty of literary quality in it. I'm starting to wonder if there might be something about the Russian language that makes good texts translate as being more poorly written than they actually are? As I got further into the book, the language seemed to improve, but maybe I just got used to it? I'm not sure.<br /><br />This is the first book of a loosely connected trilogy, (I don't think the others have been translated yet) and its ending is intense and a bit messy. But I don't think it's a badly written or rushed ending, like some of the other reviewers seem to think. The last school year described in the book is a lot less detailed than the first ones, but there is, naturally, less to describe at this point, since Sasha isn't new in town anymore. For me, the ending worked just fine. And the last 70 pages or so became very weird and intense, which I really liked. For such a big and slow paced book, it did a great job keeping me hooked all the way through. I also liked the bleakness, and the fact that the book can be quite disturbing, but I don't mean that in a trigger-warning way, but more in a brain bending "what is reality" and "what constitutes a human being" kind of way. Loved that. It's definitely a five star read for me.<br /><br />Oh, and please never try the creepy visualization exercises described in this book. They are probably bad for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11547650441825713420" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_3102382366" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3102382366" />
    <a title="Renee Godding" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71792610-renee-godding"><img alt="Renee Godding" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531216161p2/71792610.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3102382366?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Renee Godding" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Renee Godding" href="/user/show/71792610-renee-godding">Renee Godding</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062694591" href="/book/show/38633526-vita-nostra">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71792610-renee-godding?shelf=ren%C3%A9e-recommends">renée-recommends</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3102382366" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16738260763014304053"><b> Rating: 4/5 stars </b><br/><br/><i>“I’m serious, Sasha: what is so important about being human? Is it because you simply haven’t experienced anything else?” </i><br/><br/>16-year old Sasha is vacationing with her mom at the beach, when she meets a mysterious man who makes her an unusual proposition. The next day Sacha suddenly finds herself vomiting up golden coins… This even kicks off an epic coming of age fantasy novel, involving a magical university (but not in the way you expect), strange characters that seem barely huma</span>
  <span id="freeText16738260763014304053" style="display:none"><b> Rating: 4/5 stars </b><br /><br /><i>“I’m serious, Sasha: what is so important about being human? Is it because you simply haven’t experienced anything else?” </i><br /><br />16-year old Sasha is vacationing with her mom at the beach, when she meets a mysterious man who makes her an unusual proposition. The next day Sacha suddenly finds herself vomiting up golden coins… This even kicks off an epic coming of age fantasy novel, involving a magical university (but not in the way you expect), strange characters that seem barely human and at times absolute mind-melting philosophy and metaphysics. <br /><br /> It’s hard for me to paint you a vivid picture of what <i>Vita Nostra</i> is like, partly because I don’t want to take away from your own experience of exploring this labyrinthine novel yourself, but partly because I lack a frame of reference to put it against. It might be due to the fact that I’m not too familiar with Russian literature, but this had none of the tropes and predictability that I’m familiar with in American/Western European fantasy. Due to that, this was wholly unique to me and left me in the dark with regards to what was happening until the end. It's unexpected, mind blowing and WEIRD, and to be honest, I'm still unsure if this is one of the most philosophically brilliant books I’ve ever read, or just the most ravingly mad one. <br />Still, in all its surrealism, it still somehow manages to capture the experience of going off to college to study for an “adult-life” and a profession you don’t completely understand quite yet perfectly. <br /><br /> This description may push some people away from it, and I think that’s fair. If you want an action packed fantasy novel to help you switch your brain off before bed: don’t go for this one. If however, you are up for a challenge and want to read a dark-academia fantasy novel like you’ve never read before: look no further.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16738260763014304053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2350601327" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2350601327" />
    <a title="Lukasz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19694853-lukasz"><img alt="Lukasz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531207601p2/19694853.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2350601327?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lukasz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lukasz" href="/user/show/19694853-lukasz">Lukasz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/16159259-vita-nostra">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19694853-lukasz?shelf=all-time-favorite">all-time-favorite</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2350601327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18044556343887380786"><blockquote>
  <i>In the beginning was the Word</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>Science, or more specifically - the school of magic - is a trope that's used a lot in fantasy literature. Not surprising. If done well it’s a joy to read. Have Diachenko’s succeeded?<br/><br/>Short answer - yes.<br/><br/><i>Vita Nostra</i> twists the trope in most unexpected ways. It’s strange, thoughtful and dark tale about metamorphosis (both physical and metaphysical) that contains coming of age elements.<br/><br/>Sasha is a regular, high-school student who spends her holidays at the seaside. She</span>
  <span id="freeText18044556343887380786" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>In the beginning was the Word</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Science, or more specifically - the school of magic - is a trope that's used a lot in fantasy literature. Not surprising. If done well it’s a joy to read. Have Diachenko’s succeeded?<br /><br />Short answer - yes.<br /><br /><i>Vita Nostra</i> twists the trope in most unexpected ways. It’s strange, thoughtful and dark tale about metamorphosis (both physical and metaphysical) that contains coming of age elements.<br /><br />Sasha is a regular, high-school student who spends her holidays at the seaside. She enjoys the sun, fresh morning breeze, chill and happy atmosphere. Things change when a strange man with dark glasses approaches her and asks her to get up at 4 AM, go to the beach and swim naked to the buoy and back. Every single day.<br /><br />In the beginning, Sasha wants to ignore the lunatic, but it turns out that she just can’t. For many reasons. When she finally agrees and does her first 4 AM swim a bizarre thing happens – she vomits golden coins. She does it every day after her morning training. Things develop a bit from here, and soon she finds herself forced to enter the Institute of Special Technologies in Torpa. It’s an Institute in the middle of nowhere. Students aren’t told what’s the purpose of their studies. However, a failure in school isn’t an option. When it happens, a students’ loved ones pay the price.<br /><br />The students' fate remains unknown to them, but it seems they’ll change in significant and unexpected ways. For example, some of them will grow wings. Temporarily. And if you think it’s bizarre, you know nothing about bizarre. Happily, Dyachenko’s will educate you in a mind-blowing way.<br /><br />Students at the University are regular (at least in the beginning) teenagers anxious about growing up. They drink, smoke and have sex. They were selected for this school for reasons they don’t understand, forced to come to Torpa and pursue impossible and incomprehensible classes that will allow them to gain a degree they know nothing about.<br /><br />As a reader, you’re not in a better situation than students. Lack of information or at least some hints about studies' goal may be frustrating. We learn fast that students play with the fabric of the reality but how is a question I asked myself multiple times. Is it magic? What are they becoming? On the other hand, it's also one of the reasons why this novel is so fascinating and immersive. It's told in a way that allowed me to enter the world smoothly and imagine and experience impossible things. Occasional flashes of humor surface from time to time, but it’s not a joyful tale. Characters have few choices, and their existence serves a higher purpose known only be their mysterious and severe teachers. <br /><br />The storytelling is influenced by modernism, and magical realism works. The story happens in the modern era - telephones and cell phones exist in this place but aren't as readily available as in real life. Torpa is in the middle of nowhere, and its inhabitants are just sketched. They don't play any significant role in the story.<br /><br />Dyachenko's focus on their characters' psychology and inner metamorphosis. They touch philosophical and metaphysical questions with finesse and skill that made me reread the ending five times.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Why is being human so vital to you? Isn't it because you don't know anything else?</i>
</blockquote><br /> <br />This book crafts wholly new vision of the world and the laws that govern it. As the story progresses, Dyachenko's share some insights into the world metaphysics and if you've ever been fascinated with the language and power of the words, you'll be satisfied with some of the discoveries.<br /><br />I could go on, but I won't. Instead, I'll tell you this - it's one of the best books I've ever read. It combines fascinating adventure with philosophical depth, impossible metamorphosis with profound psychological insights. <b>It's strange. It's amazing. It's brilliant.</b><br /><br /><b>Side note:</b> I read Polish translation of the book. It seems there used to be an English version, but it's no longer available. Good news is that Tor will release a professional translation of <i>Vita Nostra</i> in November 2018.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18044556343887380786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2579356059" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2579356059" />
    <a title="Lou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45941903-lou"><img alt="Lou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517829400p2/45941903.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2579356059?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lou" href="/user/show/45941903-lou">Lou</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/41451467-vita-nostra">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2579356059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer718329619330200571">Vita Nostra, first published in 2007 in Ukraine, tells the story of Alexandra (Sasha) Samokhina, who whilst vacationing with her mother meets Farit Kozhennikov, a strange and sinister-looking man who forces her to attend a remote and mysterious university. This novel follows her adventures and exploits. One word that cannot be used to describe this novel is boring. It is certainly far from that and combines many different genres to create a truly original story. While I did enjoy aspects, I, unf</span>
  <span id="freeText718329619330200571" style="display:none">Vita Nostra, first published in 2007 in Ukraine, tells the story of Alexandra (Sasha) Samokhina, who whilst vacationing with her mother meets Farit Kozhennikov, a strange and sinister-looking man who forces her to attend a remote and mysterious university. This novel follows her adventures and exploits. One word that cannot be used to describe this novel is boring. It is certainly far from that and combines many different genres to create a truly original story. While I did enjoy aspects, I, unfortunately, found it rather perplexing and surreal, so much so that for the majority of the time I had no inclination about what was actually happening! However, there was something stopping me from just giving up and moving on to something else. <br /><br />As a fan of Haruki Murakami and his intensely descriptive prose, I also appreciated that here, although those who don't enjoy that style of writing may wish to avoid this, as it means that the story is quite static and takes rather a long time to come to fruition. In the end, the story does make sense, and I respected the attempt at making it a unique title that readers will remember for a long time. By the time the conclusion came, I was very sad to be leaving this fantastical, fictional world and can now absolutely understand why this book has a cult following! I suspect I will pick this up again and see what sort of impression it leaves on me after the second time. Intriguing, extraordinary, well written, wonderfully atmospheric and thoroughly unforgettable. If you enjoy fantasy, I urge you to give this a go!<br /><br />Many thanks to HarperVoyager for an ARC. I was not required to post a review, and all thoughts and opinions expressed are my own.</span>
  <a data-text-id="718329619330200571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2377975145" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2377975145" />
    <a title="unknown" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2863914-unknown"><img alt="unknown" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579703867p2/2863914.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2377975145?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="unknown" class="user" itemprop="url" name="unknown" href="/user/show/2863914-unknown">unknown</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062694591" href="/book/show/38633526-vita-nostra">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2377975145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8644118796611530703">A novel that will make your head hurt if you think too much about what it <i>seems</i> to be about. Better to concentrate on what it is actually about, which is that liminal period in your early 20s when you begin to transition into an adult, and realize how utterly terrifying and final that transformation is.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SYik0YIJ5ujTPm+WC+YQnmJT6l4xP4BnVoH2I9Alsgnn/hF3FlHrlhwBQdavw5FLLAizogCCVrywtiD5rSVi8w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('oZoGBOYAu5BhHbvx01V1BD/EcvsuRS1sBbNnTwN2YzsP7LOicli27q4ilbF3cPTRcZ8rBx/4+7fjhLGVfnazwQ==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer4002915044364066250">Марина Дяченко<br/>Marina and Sergey Dyachenko - co-authors of novels, short fiction, plays and scripts. They primarily write in Russian (and in the past also in Ukrainian) with several novels translated into English and published in the United States. These include, Vita Nostra (2012), The Scar (2012), The Burned Tower (2012), and Age of Witches (2014). The primary genres of their books are modern spe</span>
  <span id="freeText4002915044364066250" style="display:none">Марина Дяченко<br />Marina and Sergey Dyachenko - co-authors of novels, short fiction, plays and scripts. They primarily write in Russian (and in the past also in Ukrainian) with several novels translated into English and published in the United States. These include, Vita Nostra (2012), The Scar (2012), The Burned Tower (2012), and Age of Witches (2014). The primary genres of their books are modern speculative fiction, fantasy, and literary tales.</span>
